FRANK  MOORE  EFT  AL.  VS.  EF.  H,  DEMPSEY,  FT*.

1

In the  District  Court  of  the  United  States  for  the  Western  Division

of  the  Eastern  District  of  Arkansas.

Be it remembered,  That  on  the  2st  day  of  September,  A.D.  1921,
came  into  the  office  of  the  Clerk  of  the  District  Court  of  the  United
States  for  the  Western  Division  of  the  Eastern  District  of  Arkansas,
Frank  Moore,  Ed,  Hieks,  J.  2.  Knox,  Ed,  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall,
by Murphy,  MeTaney  &  Dunaway,  and  Seipio  A,  Jones,  Esqs.,  their
attorneys,  and  filed  therein  their  Petition  for  Writ  of  Habeas  Corpus,
against  EL  H.  Dempsey,  Keeper  of  the  State  Penitentiary  of  the
State  of  Arkansas,  which  Petition  for  Writ  of  Habeas  Corpus  is  as
follows :

In the  United  States  District  Court  for  the  Western  Division  of  the

Eastern  Distriet  of  Arkansas.

Frank  Moork,  Ep  Hicks,  J.  2.  Kxox,  Ep  Cotemayx,  and  Pau

Haw,  Petitioners,

Vs.

Ef. H.  Dempsey,  Keeper  of  the  Arkansas  Penitentiary,  Defendant.

Petition  fora  Writ  of Habeas  Corpus.

Your  petitioners,  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ed  Cole-
man  and  Paul  Hall,  state  that  they  are  citizens  and  residents  of  the
State  of  Arkansas,  and  are  now  residing  in  Little  Rock,  contined  ip
the  Arkansas  State  Penitentiary.  in  the  Western  Divisien  of  the
Fastern  District  of  Arkansas,  within  the  jurisdiction  of  this  court:
that  the  defendant  is  the  keeper  of  the  said  Arkansas  State  Peni-
ntiary,  and  as  such  is  unlawfully  restraining  vour  petitioners  of
their liberty,  and  will,  unless  prevented  from:  doing  so  by the  issuance
ifthe  write  herein  praved  for,  deprive  them  of  their  lives  on  the  22rd
day of Sept.  1921,  in  violation  of  the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the
United  States,  and  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  State  of  Arkansas.
Petitioners  further  say  that  they  are  negroes,  of  African  descent.
black  in color,  and  that  prior  to  the  tines  hereinafter  mentioned  were
itzens  and  residents  of  Phillips  County,  Arkansas,  at  Elaine:  that
on  the  —  day  of  October,  1919,  they  were  arrested,  placed  in  the
Phillips  County  jail  and  thereafter  until  their  trial  were  kept  in  elose
wnfinement  upon  an  alleged  charge  of  murder  in  the  first  degree  for
the killing  of  one  Clinton  Lee.  a  white  man.  said  to  have  occurred
m  the  Ist  day  of  October,  1919:  that  said  Clinton  Lee  was  killed,
they  ave  informed,  while  a  member  of  a  posse  of  white  men  who
rere  said  to  be  attempting  to  quell  a  race  riot,  growing  out  of  the
killing  of  W.  A,  Adkins  on  the  night  of  September  30th,  1919:  at
Hoop Spur,  in  said  County  and  State;  that  said  Adkins  was  killed,
they are  advised,  under  these  circumstances  and  conditions:  Peti-
Ped—595

—_—

RENE BR

I  HE

2

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL.  VS.  EL.  HW.  DEMPSEY,  ETC,

tioners  and  a  large  number  of  the  members  of  their  race  were  peace.
ably  and  lawfully  assembled  in  their  church  house  at  or  near  Hog
Spur,  with  no  unlawful  purpose  in  view,  and  with  no  desire  or  pur.
pose  to  injure  or  do  any  wrong  to  any  one;  that  while  they  wer
thus  assembled,  white  persons  began  firing  guns  or  pistols  from  the
outside  into  and  through  said  chureh  house,  through  the  windows
and  shooting  the  lights  out  therein,  causing  a  great  disturbance  and
stampede  of  those  assembled  therein:  that  the  white  persons  so  firing
on  said  Church  come  there  in  antomobiles,  of  which  there  were  se.
eral,  and  came  for  the  purpose  of  breaking  up  said  meeting:  that
said  Adkins  was  killed  either  by  members  of  his  own  party  or  by
some  other  person  unknown  to  your  petitioners:  that  the  white
men  sent  out  the  word  to  Helena.  the  County  seat,  that  said  Adkins
had  been  killed  by  the  Neoroes,  shot  down  in  cold  blood  while  on
a  peaceable  mission,  by  ca  armed  force  of  negroes,  assembled  at  said
church,  which  caused  great  excitement  all  over  the  City  of  Helena
and  Phillips  County  >  that  the  report  of  said  killing  spread  like  wild
fire  into  other  counties,  all  over  the  State  of  Arkansas,  and  into  other
States,  notably  the  State  of  Mississippi:  that  early  the  next  day a  large
number  of  white  men  of  said  county  armed  themselves  and  rushed
to  the  scene  of  the  trouble  and  to  adjacent  regions,  the  vicinity  of
Flaine  being  one  of  them,  and  began  the  indiscriminate  hunting
down,  shooting  and  killing  of  negroes;  that  in  a  short  time  white
men  from  adjoining  counties  and  from  the  State  of  Mississippi
likewise  armed  themselves,  rushed  to  the  scene  of  the  trouble  and
began  the  indiseriminate  shooting  down  of  Neeroes,  both  men  and
women,  particularly  the  posse  from  the  State  of  Mississippi,  whe
shot  down  in  cold  blood  innocent  Negro  men  and  women,  many  of
whom  were  at  the  time  in  the  fields  picking  cotton:  that  highly
inflam-atory  articles  were  published  in  the  press  of  Arkansas  and
especially  of  Helena  and  throughout  the  United  States,  in  which  the
trouble  was  variously  called  a  “race  riot.”  “and  uprising  of  the
Negroes”  and  “A  deliberately  planned  insurrection  among  the  Ne
groes  against  the  whites”  of  that  part  of  Phillips  County:  that  the
officers  of  Phillips  County,  especially  the  Sheriff,  called  upon  the
Governor  ef  the  State.  and  the  Governor  in  turn  called  upon  the
Commanding  Officer  at  Camp  Pike  for  a  large  number  of  United
States  soldiers  to  assist  the  citizens  in  quelling  the  so  called  “race
riot,”  “uprising”  or  “insurrection”;  that  a  company  of  soldiers  was
dispatched  to  the  scene  of  the  trouble  who  took  charee  of  the  situa
tion  and  finaily  saceeeded  in  stopping  the  slaughter,

Your  petitioners  further  say  that  they,  together  with  a  large  num-
ber  of  their  race,  both  men  and  women,  were  taken  to  the  Phillips
County  jail,  at  Helena,  incarcerated  therein,  and  charged  with  mur
der;  that  a  committee  of  seven  composed  of  leading  Helena  busines
men  and  officials,  to-wit:  Sebastian  Straub,  Chairman,  H.  D.  Moore,
County  Judge,  F.  F.  Kitchens,  Sheriff,  J.  G.  Knight,  Mayor,  E. M.
Allen,  J.  FE.  Horner  and  T.  W.  Keesee,  was  selected  for  the  purpose
of  probing  into  the  situation  and  picking  out  those  to  be  condemned
to  death  and  those  to  be  condenmmed  and  sentenced  to  the  peniten-
tiary;  that  said  Committee  assumed  charge  of  the  matter  and  pre

hem,  cutting  the  blood  at  every  lick  until  the  victim:  would  agree  to

ilso provided  in  said  jail,  to  further  frighten  and  torture  therm,  an
Jectric  chair,  in  which  they  would  be  put  naked  and  the  curre:t
wmed  on  to  shock  and  frighten  them  into  giving  damaging  state-
yents  against  themselves  and  others;  also  strangling  drugs  were  put
p their  noses  for  the  same  purpose  and  by these  methods  and  means
false  evidence  was  extorted  from  Negroes  to  be  used  and  was  used
ainst  your  petitioners.

Petitioners  further  say  that  on  every  day  from:  October  Ist.  until
iter their  trial  on  November  3rd.  1919,  the  press  of  Helena  and  the
state  of Arkansas  carried  inflam-atory  articles  giving  accounts  of  the
rouble,  which  were  calculated  to  arouse  and  did  arouse  bitter  feeling
wainst  your  petitioners  and  the  other  members  of  their  race:  that  ou
(etober  Tth,  1919,  the  Helena  World.  a  newspaper  published  and
printed  in  the  City  of  Helena,  and  having  a  wide  and  almost  uni-
vasal  circulation  throughout  said  County,  published  an  article  writ-
wn  and  given  out  by Mr.  BE.  M.  Allen,  a  member  of  said  Committee
if Seven,  for  and  on  behalf  of  said  Committee,  purporting  to  give
the facts  concerning  what  he  called  “not  a  race  riot.”  but  a  ‘“delib-
aately  planned  insurrection  of  the  Negroes  against  the  Whites.”  in
vhich  it was  stated  that  the  Union,  of  which  petitioners  were  mem-
ies,  was  “established  for  the  purpose  of  banding  Negroes  together
for the  killing  of  white  people”;  that  a  copy  of  said  said  article  is
ittached  hereto,  made  a  part  hereof  and  marked  Exhibit  “A™:  that
tortly  after  being  placed  in  Jail,  a  mob  was  formed  in  the  eity  of
Helena,  composed  of  hundreds  of  men,  who  marched  to  the  County
wil for the  purpose  and  with  the  intent  of  lynching  vour  petitioners
ind others,  and  would  have  done  so  but  for  the  interference  of  United
‘ates  soldiers  and  the  promise  of  seme  of  said  Committee  and  other
vading  officials  that  if  the  mob  would  stay  its  hand  they  would  ex-
ute  those  found  guilty  in  the  form  of  law.

Petitioners  further  state  that  prior  to  October  1.  1919,  they  were
farmers,  share  croppers;  that  nearly  all  the  land  in  Phillips  County
sowned  by white  men;  that  same  is  rented  out  to  share  croppers  to
tilled  on  shares,  one  half  to  the  tenant  and  the  other  half  to  the
wher;  that  for  some  years  past  there  has  grown  up  a  sv-tem  among
he land  owners  of  furnishing  the  Negro  tenants  supplies  on  whieh
make  crops  and  which  is caleulated  to  deprive  and  does  deprive  the
Negro tenants  of all  their  interest  in  the  crops  produced  by them;  that
‘pursuance  of  this  system,  they  refused  to  give  their  share  eroppers
y itemized  statement  of  account  of  their  indebtedness  for  supplies
Murnished,  refused  to  let  them  move  or  sell  any  part  of their  crops,

soos ay

4

PRANK  MOORE  ET  AL,  VS.  EL  1.  DEMPSEY,  ETC,

but  themselves  sell  and  dispose  of  the  same  at  such  prices  as  they
please,  and  then  give  to  the  Negroes  no  account  thereof,  pay  then
only  such  amount  as  they  wish  and  in  this  way  kept  them  down,
poverty  stricken  and  effectually  under  their  control;  that  for  the pur.
pose  of  protecting  themselves,  if  possible  against  the  oppressive  and
ruimous  effects  of  this  system,  the  Negro  farmers  organized  societies
with  the  view  of  uniting  their  financial  resourees  in  moral  and  legal
teasures  fo  overcome  the  same,  which  fact  heeame  quickly  known to
the  plantation  owners:  that  such  owners  were  bitterly  opposed  to such
societies,  sought  to  prevent  their  organization,  ordered  the  members
to  discontinue  their  meetings  and  sought  by every  means  they  eould
employ  to  disrupt  them:  that  on  the  30th  day  of  September,  1919.
petitioners  and  other  members  of  the  Ratio  Lodge,  near  Elaine.
learned  that  some  of  the  Negro  farmers  of  a  nearby  plantation  had
emploved  UL  S.  Bratton,  an  attorney  of  Litthe  Rock,  Arkansas,  to
represent  them  in  effecting  a  settlement  for  them:  with  their  land
lords,  or  if  he  eould  not  so  institute  leval  proceedings  to  protect  their
interests,  and  that  either  he.  or  his  representatives,  would  be  there  ou
the  following  day  to  meet  with  all  the  parties  concerned,  perfect  the
vrrangements,  and  learn  all  the  facts  as  far  as  possible,  and  decided  to
hold  a  meeting  with  the  view  of  seeing  him  while  there,  and  engag-
ing  him  as  an  attorney  to  protect  their  interest;  that  accordingly  they
met  that  night  at  Hloop  Spur  Chureh  house,  which  resulted,  as  here
inbefore  set  out,  in  the  killing  of  said  Adkins  and  the  breaking  up
of  said  meeting:  that  on  the  morning  of  October  Ist,  Mr.  O.  8.  Brat-
ton,  son  and  agent  of  Attorney  U.S.  Bratton,  arrived  in  Elaine  for
consultation  with  those  who  might  desire  to  employ  his  father,  was
arrested,  barely  escaped  being  mobbed,  notwithstanding  it  was  well
known.  he  was  there  only  for  the  purpose  of  advising  with  those
Negroes  as  to  their  rights,  and  getting  from  them  such  facts  as  would
enable  his  father  intelligently  to  prepare  for  their  legal  rights:  that
he  was  carried  thence  to  the  County  jail,  thrown  into  it  and  kept
closely  contined  on  a  charge  of  murder  until  the  31st  day  of  the
same  month,  when  he  was  jndieted  on  a  charge  of  barratry,  without
any  evidence  to  sustain  the  charge;  that  on  that  day,  he  was  told  by
officials  that  he  would  be  discharged,  but  not  to  go  on  the  public
streets  anvwhere,  to  keep  the  matter  a  secret,  to  leave  secretly  ina
closed  automobile  and  to  go  to  West  Helena,  four  miles  away,  and
aveid  being  mobbed;  that  he  was  told
there  take  the  train,  so  as  to
that  he  would  be  mobbed,  or  would  be  in  great  danger  of  being
mobbed  if  his  release  became  known  publicly  before  he  was  out  of
reach:  that  the  Judge  of  the  Cireuit  Court,  the  Judge  of  the  same
court  before  whom  petitioners  were  tried,  facilitated  the  secret  de-
parture  and  himself  went  to  West  Helena  and  there  remained  until
he  had  seen  said  Bratton  safely  on  the  train  and  the  train  departed.

witnesses  alone:  that  after  the  court's  instructions.  the  jury  retired

exeeding  two  or  three  minutes,  and  they  were  promptly  sentenced  to
death  by electrocution  for  December  27th,  191%.

Petitioners  further  say  that  during  the  course  of  said  trial,  whieh
lasted  less  than  an  hour,  that  only  two  witnesses  testified  to  anything

that  said  witnesses  were  Walter  Ward:  and  John  Jelferson,  both  of
whom  were  Negroes  and  were  under  indictment  at  the  same  time  for
the killing  of  said  Lee:  that  they  were  compelled  to  testify:  against
them  by the  same  methods  and  means  hereinbefore  described:  that
their  testimony  was  wholly  false  and  that  they  gave  such  testimony

to testify  that  they  would  be  killed.  but  that  if  they  did  -o  testify,
and  would  plead  guilty  their  punishment  would  be  light;  that  they
thereafter  plead  guilty  to  murder  in  the  second  degree  and  were

such  that  they  could  net
white  jury  of  said  county,  vet  he  filed  no  petition  for  a  change  of

nothing  to  protect  their  interests;  that  the  court  did  not  ask  thew
whether  they  had  counsel,  or  desired  to  employ  counsel,  or  were  able
to  do  so,  but  simply  said  a  lawyer,  whom  he  named,  would  defen
them:  that  they  lave,  therefore.  not  had  a  trial,  have  had  no
opportunity  to  make  a  defense,  but  that  their  case  was  closed  agains
them  as  virtually  and  effectually  as  if  on  a  plea  of  guilty:  that  if
they  had  been  given  the  opportunity  they  would  have  emploved
counsel  of  their  own  choice  and  have  made  a  defense,  their  ability
to  do  so  having  been  demonstrated  since  their  conviction:  that  the
feeling  against  petitioners  was  such  that  it  over-awed  the  Judge  on
the  bench,  the  jury,  the  attorney  appointed  to  defend  them  and  every
one  connected  with  said  court;  that  all.  Judge,  jury  and  counsel  wen
dominated  by  the  mob  spirit  that  was  universally  present  in  contr
and  out.  so  that  if  anv  juror  had  had  the  courage  to  investigate  said
charge  with  any  spirit  of  fairness,  and  vote  for  an  acqu-ttal,  he,  hin-
self.  would  have  been  the  victim  of  the  mob;  that  such  was  the
intensity  of  feeling  against  petitioners  and  the  other  defendants  that
had  counsel  for  them  objected  to  the  testimony  of  the  two  witness:
against  them,  said  Ward  and  Jefferson,  on  the  ground  that  it was
extorted  by  beating  and  torture,  as  they  are  advised  he  should  have
done,  he  himself  would  have  been  the  vietim  of  the  mob:  that  it i:
possible  counsel  did  not  know  how  the  evidence  against  them  was
obtained,  and  they  do  not  desire  to  appear  to  ertticize  him,  yet  he
knew  that  if the  evidence  against  them  was  acquired  as  before  stated.
it  was  incompelent  and  should  have  been  excluded,  a  facet  which
petitioners  did  not  know:  that  petitioners  were  ignorant  of  their
rights,  had  never  been  in  court  before.  and  had  counsel  asked  then
about  this  testimony  they  would  have  told  him  how  it was  obtained:
that  through  fear  of  the  mob  spirit  no  witness  was  called  in  their
behalf  and  they  themselves  were  advised  not  to  take  the  stand  en
their  own  behalf;  that  as  a  result  of  the  mob  domination  of  court,
counsel  and  jury,  the  court,  although  a  court  of  original  jurisdiction
in  felony  cases,  lost  its  jurisdiction  by  virtue  of  such  mob  domination
and  the  result  was  but  an  empty  ceremony,  carried  through  in  the
apparent  form  of  law,  and  that  the  verdict  of  the  jury  was  really  a
mob  verdiet,  dictated  by  the  spirit  of  the  mob  and  pronounced  and
returned  because  no  other  verdiet  would  have  been  tolerated,  and
that  the  judement  avainst  then  is, therefore,  a  nullity.

Petitioners  further  say  that.  ever  since  the  law  of  Arkansas  for
the selection  of  jury  commissioners,  the  grand  and  petit  jurors,  as
t now  stands,  was  enacted,  all  of  the  judges  of  the  courts  have  been
and are  now  white  men,  and  that  ever  since  then  said  judges  have
appointed,  without  exception,  white  commissioners  to  select  the
qrors,  both  grand  and  petit,  and  that  such  commissioners  have  uni-
formally  selected  only  white  men  on  such  juries;  that  all  of  this
has been  done  in  discrimination  against  the  negro  race,  on  account
of their  color;  that  such  has  been  the  unbroken  practice  in  Phillips
County  for  more  than  thirty  years.  notwithstanding  the  negro
population  in  said  county  exceeds  the  white  population  by  more
than  five  to  one  and  that  a  large  proportion  of  them  are  electors  and
possess  the  legal,  moral  and  intellectual  qualifications  required  or
necessary  for such  jurors;  that  the  exclusion  of  said  negroes  from  the
juries  was,  at  all  times,  intentional  and  because  of  th:  tr  color,  of
their  being  negroes;  that  such  was  the  case  of  the  grand  jury  by
which  petitioners  were  indicted,  and  of  the  petit  jury  that  pro-
nounced  them  guilty;  that  under  the  law  of  Arkansas.  as  construed
by the  Supreme  Court  of  the  State,  an  objection  to  an  indietment
on  the  ground  that  it  was  found  by a  grand  jury  composed  only  of

to the petit  jury  must  be  made  before  a  plea  is entered  to  the  indict-
ment:  that  at  the  time  said  indictment  was  found  petitioners  were
confined  in  jail  and  did  not  know  the  grand  jury  had  heen  organ-
ined, did  not  know  it  was  in  session,  did  not  know  they  were  to  he

Petitioners.  therefore,  say  that  by  the  proceedings  aforesaid,  they
were  deprived  of  their  rights  and  are  about  to  be  deprived  of  their
lives  in  violation  of  Section  1,  of  the  14th  Amendment  of  the  Cop.
stitution  of  the  United  States  and  the  laws  of  the  United  States  ep.
acted  in  pursuance  therete,  in  that  they  have  been  denied  the  equal
protection  of  the  law,  and  have  been  convicted,  condemned,  and  ar
about  to  be  deprived  of  their  lives  without  due  process  of  law:  tha
they  are  now  in  the  custody  of  the  defendant,  E.  TL.  Detagsy,  Keeper
of  the  Arkansas  State  Penitentiary,  to  he  electrocuted  on  the  23n)
day  of  September,  1921;  that  they  are  now  detained  and  held  in,
custody  by  said  Keeper  and  will  be  eleetrocuted  on  said  date  unles
prevented  from  so  doing  by  the  issuance  of  a  writ  of  Habeas  Corpus
Petitioners  therefore  pray  that  a  Writ  of  Habeas  Corpus  be  issued
to  the  end  that  they  may  be  discharged  from  said  unlawful  imprison:
ment  and  unlawful  judgment  and  sentence  to  death.

(Signed  )

The  Committee  of  Seven.

The  Committee  of  Seven,  chosen  to  direct  operations  in  putting
down  the  insurrection  and  to  conduct  investigations  with  the  view
to discovering  and  punishing  the  guilty,  is  composed  of  the  follow-
ing citizens  :

Sebastian  Straub,  Chairman.
H. D.  Moore,  County  Judge.
F. F.  Kitchens,  Sheriff.
J.G.  Knight,  Mavor.
E. M.  Allen.
E. C.  Horner.
T. W.  Keese.

All  persons,  white  or  black,  in  possession  of  information  which
might  assist  in  discovering  and  running  down  those  responsible  for
the  insurrection  are  urgently  requested  to  confer  with  the  Commit-
tee  asa  Whole  or  with  some  individual  member  thereof,  it  being  un-
derstood  that  such  action  will  be  for  the  public  safety  and  that  the
informant’s  identity  will  he  carefully  safeguarded.

Itis the  Duty  of  Every  Citizen  in  Possession  of  Such  Information  to

Present  it  to  the  Committee.

The  following  statement  was  given  to  the  press  here  late  yesterday
afternoon  by  the  President
FE.  M.  Allen  of  the  Business  Men’s
league  and  the  Commnittee  of  Seven,  but  for  some  reason  mot  ex-
plained  editors  of  various  papers  to  which  the  statement  was  sent
aw  fit  to  substitute  a  different  “lead.”  in  most  cases  failing  com-
pletely  to  identify  Mr.  Allen.

Now  the  “ignorance  and  superstition  of  a  race  of  children”  was
played  upon  for  momentary  gain  and  for  the  banding  together  of
Negroes  to  slay  whites,  was  authoritatively  revealed  in  a  statement
sted  here  vesterday  by  E.  M.  Allen,  a  member  of the  Committee  of
‘even,  who  has  heard  virtually  all  the  prisoners’  confessions  follwing
lst week's  uprising  in  this  section  of  Arkansas.

The  Committee  of  Seven  is  composed  of  leading  Helena  Business
Ma.  It has  been  authorized  to  carry  on  the  investigation  both  by

the  municipal  and  county  authorities  and  by  Governor  Charles

ties.

Among  its  members  is  Sheriff  FL  FB.  Kitchens.

It  is  co-operating  with  the  military  author.

Mr.  Allen  is considered  by  leading  officials  and  citizens  of  Helen
as  being  most  competent  to  speak  on  the  subject  because  of  his
prominent  part  in  the  investigations  which  have  taken  place.
He
has  been  a  resident  of  Helena  for  thirteen  vears  and  was  one  of  the
men  who  originally  plotted  and  created  Elaine,  center  of  the  recent
disorders.

Aside  from  being  locally  prominent  as  president  of  the  Helen,
Business  Men’s  League,  and  as  the  owner  of  considerable  property,
of  the  National  Association  of  Tnsurance
Mr.  Allen  is  Presidnet
Agents,  the  headquarter.  of  which  are  in’  New  York  City.
His
name  was  on  the  list  of  those  the  negroes  plotted  to  kill  vesterday  a:
the  signal  for  a  general  slaughter  of  the  whites,  according  to  paper
contiseated  and  confessions  of  leaders  captured.

Mr.  Allen's  statement  follows:

“The  present  trouble  with  the  negroes  in  Phillips  County  is not  «
race  riot,  It  is  a  deliberately  planned  insurrection  of  the  negroes
against  the  white-,  directed  by  an  organization  known  as  the  “Pro.
gressive  Farmers  and  TLousehold  Union  of  \merica.”  established
for  the  purpose  of  banding  negroes  together  for  the  killing  of  white
This  “union”  was  started  by  Robert  L.  Till,  a  negro  4
people.
in  ut  an  opportunity
vears  old,  of  Winchester,  Arkansas,  whe  saw
of  making  easy  money.
He  had  been  a  farmer  all  his  life,  buy
lately  had  been  posing  as  a  “private  detective  doing  werk  in  this
and  all  foreign  countries.”

“He  started  his  first  ‘union’  work  in  April  of  this  vear.

He  or.
ganized  the  Ratio  Lodge  in  May  of  this  vear.
Tle  chose  Ratio  be.
THe  told  the  darkies
cause  his  mother  happened  to  be  living  there.
that  he  was  an  agent  of  the  government  and  beeause  the  senators
and  representatives  at  Washington  were  white  men  and  in  svmpathy
with  the  white  men  of  the  south,  it  was  impossible  for  the  negroes
to  get  the  rights  that  had  been  promised  them:  for  service  in  the
army,  and  so  the  government  had  called  into  existence  this  organiz-
tion  which  would  be  supported  by  the  government  in  defense  of thi
negroes  against  the  white  people.
Tle  told  them:  it  was  necessary
for  all  members  of  the  Union  to  arm  themselves  in  preparation  for
the  day  when  they  should  he  called  upon  to  attack  their  white  op:
pressors,

“The  slogan  of  the  organization  is  “We  battle  for  our  rights.”

The  password  of  wi  the  lodges  was  “We  have  just  begun.”

“Te  told  them  that  those  members  who  where  unable  to  buy  mu
nitions  would  be  supplied  by  the  union  from:  the  government  store:
house  at  Winchester.

.

“The  purely  mercenary  side  of  all  develops  as  follows:  Negro  men
were  charged  $1.50  entrance  fees  and  Negro  women  50  cents.
At
the  second  and  third  meeting  he  would  bring  Dr.  V.  FE.  Powell  of
Winchester  with  him,  who  purported  to  be  the  examining  physician

Affidavit  of George  Green.

STATE  OF  ARKANSAS,

County  of Lincoln:

George  Green  being  first  duly  sworn,  deposes  and  says:
My  name  is  George  Green.

[ am  now  22  years  of  age.  and  an
confined  in  the  State  Penitentiary  on  a  convict  farm  at  Cummins
Arkansas.
IT am  the  same  George  Green  who  testified  for  the  State
in  the  case  of  the  State  of  Arkansas  vs.  Frank  Hieks,  who  was  triel
on  November  3,  1919,  in  the  Phillips  Cireuit  Court,  on  a  charge d
murder  in  the  first  degree  for  having  killed  Clinton  Lee.
On  that
trial  I testified  that  Frank  Hicks  was  at  the  lodge  meeting  on  Thur
day  night  before  this  trouble  came  up  on  the  following  Tuesday
and  had  his  gun  there.
|  alse  testified  that  everybody  | saw  had
| further  more  testified  that  on  Wednesday  1 took  my
guns  there.
shot  gun  and  went  over  to  Frank  Moosre’s  house  a  little  before  dav
light  and  that  there  were  about  thirty  people  there  sitting  around.
all  of  them  had  e@uns.  and  that  about  10  or  11  o’elock  there  were
about  seventy-five  there  and  we  all  leet  ineluding  the  defendant.
Frank  Hieks,  and  marched  away  with  Frank  Moore  in  command  t
go  over  towards  the  place  where  we  heard  shooting;  and  that  when
we  crossed  the  railroad  and  had  gotten  in  the  public  road  Frank
Hicks  fired  two  shots,  and  that  when  he  went  to  shoot  the  second
time  T threw  up  my  hands,  and  says.  “Bovs  don’t  shoot  ;” that he
shot  straight  up  the  road  towards  MeCoy’s  house,  which  was

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL.  VS.  FE.  H.  DEMPSEY,  ETC.

17

that  he shot  in  the  direction  of some  automobiles,  and  that  after  that
I went  home.

I now  state  and  sweer  positive,  that  the  testimony  was  false  from
beginning  to end,  and  that I testified  as  | did  because  I was  compelled
todo  so.
I furthermore  state  that  I was  not  at  Hoop  Spur  on  the
night  the  trouble  started,  and  that  the  first  I knew  about  it  was  the
next  day when  I heard  that  the  white  folks  were  down  in  that  country
to kill  out  all  us  negroes,  and  that  | did  nothing  except  to  keep  out
of the  way.
I was  arrested  on  Saturday  after  the  trouble  started  on
Tuesday,  was  taken  to  Helena,  placed  in  jail  and  kept  in  close  con-
Shortly  after  I was  taken  to  Helena  I was
finement  until  court  met.
taken  out  of jail  and  into  a  room  where  three  men  examined  me.
I
do not  know  who  these  men  were,  but  they  told  me  I had  to  testify
against  Frank  Hicks.  They  would  ask  me  questions,  and  I told  them
I didn’t  know  anything  about  the  matter  and  they  would  curse  me,
abuse  me,  telling  me  I did  know,  that  I had  to  tell  it,  and  that  if  I
1 was  not  whipped,  but  a  great  many
didn’t  tell  they  would  kill  me.
of the negroes  there  in jail  with  me  were  whipped.
They  would  take
aman  out  of  the  jail  and  into  an  adjoining  room  and  we  could  hear
the  licks  as  they  whipped  him,  and  when  they  would  bring  the  man
A  great  many  men  were
back  blood  would  be  running  from  him.
taken  out  and  whipped  and  would  be  brought  back  in  such  a  condi-
tion  that  they  were  unable  to  walk.  They  would  be  dragged  back  to
the cell  door  and  dumped  into  the  cell  and  left  for  the  other  prison-
ers  to  take  care  of  them.
Knowing  how  these  negroes  had  been
treated  and  knowing  that  if  I did  not  do  what  they  told  me  to  do
that  I would  be  punished  as  they  were,  and  in  order  to  avoid  such
punishment  I finally  agreed  to  testify  to  anything  that  they  wanted
me  tosay.
So  they  told  me  what  I had  to  say  just  as  I testified  to  it
in the  trial,  and  when  I was  called  as  a  witness  T did  what  they  told
metodo.
At  the  same  time  I was  indicted  for  the  murder  of  Clin-
ton  Lee, and  they  told  me  that  if  | would  testify  against  Frank  Hicks
and  then  plead  guilty,  that  they  would  get  the  court  to  make  it light
onme.  I later  plead  guilty  to  murder  in  the  second  degree  and  was
sentenced  to six  years  in the  penitentiary.
What  I testified  to  against
He  did  not  fire  the  shots  that  killed
Frank  Hicks  was  untrue.
I was
Clinton  Lee  or  anybody  else  at  any  time  that  I was  with  him.
not  guilty  of  having  anything  to  do  with  the  killing  of  Clinton  Lee
or  anybody  else,  and  I  only  testified  as  I  did  because  I was  com-
pelled  to.

(Signed)

GEORGE  GREEN.

Subscribed  and  sworn  to  before  me  this  18  day  of  Mav,  1921.

[SEAL. ]

(Signed)

J.  H.  ALBRIGHT,

Notary  Public.

My Commission  Expires  April  25,  1925.

Endorsed:  Filed  Sept.  21,  1921.

Sid.  B.  Redding,  Clerk.

Affidavit  of John  Jefferson.

STATE  OF  ARKANSAS,

County  of Lincoln:

This  was  the  only  meeting  T ever  attended.

John  Jefferson  being  first  duly  sworn,  deposes  and  says:
My  name  is  John  Jefferson,

Tam  30  years  of  age,  and  am  now
confined  in  the  Stute  Penitentiary,  on  the  State  Farm  at  Cummins
Arkansas,  on  a  sentence  toa  plea  of  guilty  for  murder  in  the  second
degree  for  the  killing  of  Clinton  Lee,
T first  refused  to  plead  guilty
because  Twas  not  guilty,
1 had  nothing  to  do  with  the  killing  of
Clinton  Lee,  and  know  nothing  about  who  killed  him.  — 1 was  taken
back  to  jail  and  told  that  if  T didn’t  plead  guilty  that  T would  be sen
to  the  electric  chair,  so  they  took  me  baek  to  court  again  and  | plead
euilty  and  they  gave  me  five  years,
 T plead  guilty  to  save  my  own
Thad  dived  at  Plaine  three  vears  prior  to  this  trouble  there,  |
life.
joined  the  union  on  Priday  night  before  the  trouble  on  Tuesday
night.
1 saw  no  guns
at  that  meeting  and  Thad  no  gun  myself,
Twas  arrested  on  Friday
They  took  me  to  Elaine
following  out  in  the  field  pieking  cotton.
and  kept  me  there  until  Monday  when  they  took  me  to  Telena  and
While  T was
put  mein  jail  with  a  large  number  of  other  negroes,
in  jail  they  took  a  great  many  negroes  out  of  the  jail  and  whipped
T could  hear  the  licks  and  when  they  came  back  bleeding
them.
and  unable  to  walk  and  all  whipped  to  pieces,  some  of  them  were  s
hadly  beaten  up  that  they  had  te  craw!  for  several  days,  being  unable
to  walk,  and  they  would  also  punish  them  by  putting  strangling
T heard  they  had  an  electric
drugs  to  their  noses  to  punish  them.
chair  in  jail  but  didn’t  see  it.  They  took  me  out  of  jail  three  times
and  talked  to  me  about  my  testimony,  told  me  that  T had  to  testify
against  Frank  Hicks  and  Frank  Moore  and  these  other  men,  and
that  if  I didn’t  testify  against  them  they  would  do  me  like  they did
the  others,  beat  me  up.
On  the  other  trial  T testified  against  Frank
Hicks,  and  also  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Tieks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman

STaTe  OF  ARKANSAS,

County  of Phillips:

Be it remembered  that  the  Circuit  Court  of  Phillips  County,  Ar-
kansas,  met  at  the Court  House  in the  City  of Helena,  Arkansas,  Octo-
ber 27th,  1919,  at  10  o’clock  A.  M.,,  it being  the  day  fixed  by law  for
There  was  present  and  Presiding  the  Hon.  J. M.
holding said  court.
Jackson,  Judge  of said  Court,  A.  G.  Burke,  Clerk  and  F.  F.  Kitchens,
when  after  due  proclamation  by the  Sheriff,  Court  was  opened  when
the following  proceedings  were  had  to-wit:

In  the  Matter  of the  Organization  of  the  Grand  Jury.

Now  on  this  day  comes  the  sheriff  in  open  court  and  returns  the
venire  heretofore  issued  by  the  Clerk  of  this  Court  for  the  Grand
Jurors,  which  were  selected  by the  Jury  Commissioners  at  the  April
Term, 1919,  of this  Court,  from  which  venire  the  following  qualified

.

electors  of  Phillips  County,  Arkansas,  were  duly  examined,  selected
and  sworn  as  follows,  to-wit:—C.  L.  Moore,  Jr.,  T.  W.  Keesee,  (. |
Bernard,  J. J. Pedro,  J.  W.  Denison,  James  H.  Pillow,  C. A. Wooten
Joseph  Truemper,  H.  E.  McRae,  8.  A.  Wooten  B.  A.  Dunlap  §
Straub,  Abe  Pupkin,  E.  P.  Govan,  Jr.,  and  Jas.  C.  Rembert,  and  §)
Frank  and  who  having  been  duly  sworn,  were  empanneled  to  serye
as  Grand  Jurors  at  the  present  term  of  this  court.

Thereupon  theCourtcharged  saidGrand  Jury  as  to  their  duties  and
appointed  H.  E.  McRae,  one  of  their  number  as  Foreman;  They
then  retired  to  consider  of  their  duties.

Indictment.

Phillips  Circuit  Court.

Strate  oF  ARKANSAS

against

FraNK  Moore,  Ep  Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ep  CoLeman  and

Indictment.

The  Grand  Jury  of  Phillips  County  in  the  name  and  by the av.
thority  of  the  State  of  Arkansas,  accuse  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks
J.  E.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall,  of  the  crime  of  murder
in  the  first  degree  committed  as  follows,  to-wit:  The  said  Frank
Moore,  Ed  Hicks.  J.  BE.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Tall.  in the
county  and  State  aforesaid,  on  the  Ist  day  of  October,  A.  D.  1919:
unlawfully,  wilfully,  feloniously  and  with  malice  aforethought  and
after  deliberation  and  premeditation  did  kill  and  murder  one  Clin-
which  they,  the  said  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ed Cole.
ton  Lee,  by shooting  him,  the  said  Clinton  Lee,  with  a  certain  gun
man  and  Paul  Hall,  then  and  there  had  and  held  in  their  hands,
the  said  gun  being  then  and  there  loaded  with  gunpowder  and
leaden  balls  against  the  peace  and  dignity  of  the  State  of  Arkansas,

JNO.  E.  MILLER,

Prosecuting  Attorney.

Endorsed:  No.  4495.

Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall.
E.  McRae,  Foreman.

Indictment  for  murder  in  the  first  degree,

Be it remembered,  that  on  this  4th  day  of  November,  1919,  the
dove and  foregoing  cause  coming  on  to  be  heard  before  Hon.  J.  M.
ckson,  Judge  of  the  Ist  Judicial  Cireuit  of  Arkansas,  and  a  jury
ily empaneled  to  hear  the  same  at  the  regular  November  Term.
119, of the  Phillips  Cireuit  Court,  and  the  defendants,  Frank  Moore,
i Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Paul  Hall  and  Ed  Coleman  appearing  in
json  and  by  atorneys,  J.  1.  Moore  and  Greenfield  Quarles,  and
le State  being  represented  by  Hon.  J.  FE.  Miller,  Prosecuting  At-
umey;  and  both  parties  announcing  ready  for  trial,  thereupon,
sid cause proceeded  to  trial,  and  the  following  evidence  was  intro-

here  at  Elaine?

Meyers’  car.

In  what  County  and  State  was  that?
Phillips  County,  Arkansas.

Yes  sir.
How?

Q.  How  many  bullets  did  you  hear?

to  unload  and  reload  the  gun.

A.  Yes  sir.

ogressive  Union?

Oral.
Thereupon,  the  Court  orally  instructed  the  jury  as  follows:
“Gentlemen  of  the  jury,  the  defendants,  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,
-E. Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall  have  been  indicted  by the
grand jury of your  county  charged  with  the  crime  of  murder  in  the
mst  degree,  committed  as  follows,  towit:  The  said  Frank  Moore,
Ed Hicks,  J.  E.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall,  in  the  County
and State  aforesaid,  on  the  1st  day  of  October,  A,  D.  1919,  unlaw-
fully,  wilfully,  feloniously  and  with  malice  aforethought  and  after
deliberation  and  premeditation  did  kill  and  murder  one  Clinton  Lee,
th
a certain  gun  which  they,  the  said  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,
/E)Knox,  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall,  then  and  there  had  and

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL.  VS.  THE  STATE  OF  ARKANSAS,

held  in  their  hands,  the  said  gun  being  then  and  there  loaded  with
gun  powder  and  leaden  balls.

Under  this  indictment,  gentlemen,  it  is  sufficient,  if  the
justifies  it,  to  convict  the  defendants  of  murder  in  the  first  d
or  to  convict  them  of  murder  in  the  second  degree  or  to  acquit  them,

Murder  is the  unlawful  killing  of  a  human  being  in  the  peace ¢
the  State  with  malice  aforethought  either  express  or  implied;  th
manner  of  the  killing  is  not  material,  further  than  it  may  shor
the  disposition  of  mind  or  the  intent  with  which  the  act  was  com.
Express  malice  is that  deliberate  intention  of  mind  to uw,
mitted.
lawfully  take  away  the  life  of  a  human  being,  which  is  manifeste
by  external  circumstances  capable  of  proof.
Malice  shall  be im
plied  when  no  considerable  provocation  appears,  or  when  al
the  circumstances  of  the  act  manifests  am  abandoned  @
wicked  disposition.
The  killing  being  proven,  the  burde

of  proving  circumstances  of  mitigation  that  justifies  or  exeuse- th
homicide  shall  devolve  upon  the  accused,  unless  by  the  proof  ups
the  part  of  the  prosecution  it  is sufficiently  manifest  that  the  offen
amounted  only  to  manslaughter,  or  that  the  accused  was  justifie
or  excused  in  committting  the  homicide.
All  murder  which  shal
be  perpetrated  by  means  of  poisno  or  by  lying  in  wait,  or  by any
other  kind  of  wilful,  deliberate,  malicious  or  premeditated  killing
shall  be  deemed  murder  in  the  first  degree:  all  other  murder  shal
be  deemed  murder  in  the  second  degree.
You  will  observe  that in
order  to  constitute  murder  in  the  second  degree  it  is  neccessary
show  that  the  killing  was  unlawful  and  done  with  malice  afore
thought;  it  is  immaterial  for  how  long  the  malice  existed  so  tha
it existed  and  preceded  and  caused  the  homicide.
No  deliberation
and  premeditation  is  necessary  in  murder  in  the  second  degree; t
raise  it to  murder  in  the  first  degree  there  must  not  only  be malic
aforethought,  but  the  specifie  intent  to  kill  and  premeditation  and
deliberation,  and  premeditation  means  thought  of  before  hand; d
liberation  means  the  having  in  mind  the  consequences  of  a  cours
of  conduct  as  distinguished  from  acting  on  a  sudden  impulse  with
It  is  immaterial  how  long
out  the  exercise  of  the  reasoning  powers.
the  premeditation  and  deliberation  exists  so  that  it  existed  and pre
In  other  words,  gentlemen  of  the  jury,  the dis
ceeded  the  killing.
tinction  between  murder  in  the  first  degree  and  murder  in  the se
ond  degree,  is  that  in  murder  in  the  second  degree  there  need  nd
be specifif  intent  to  take  human  life  at  the  time  the  fatal she
is  fired.
Nor  need  there  be  any  premeditation  or  deliber
tion:  that  is  the  distinction  between  murder  in  the  first  an
murder  in  the  second  degree;  in  murder  in  the  first  degree  it mus
be  done  with  malice  aforethought  and  with  premeditation  and  de
liberation,  and  with  the  specifie  intent  to  take  human  life  at th
time  the  fatal  shot  is fired.

ind from  the  evidence  in  this  case  that  the  defendants,  Frank  Moore,

hen you  Will  find  them  guilty  as  charged  in  the  indictment,  and  the

ishment  is the  same  as  the  principal.
if vou  find  from  the  evidence  in  this  case  that  the  defendants  are
ailty as  charged  in  the  indictment,  or  rather  that  some  of  the
ifendents  ae  guilty,  it  is  your  duty  to  find  the  ones  guilty  that
he evidence  shows  is  guilty  beyond  a  reasonable  doubt  and  to  ex-
gerate  or  acquit  the  ones  that  you  have  a  reasonable  doubt  as  to
their  guilt,  in  other  words,  you  may  find  all  of  the  defendanf$
ty,  or  a  part  of  them  guilty,  one  or  any  number  of  the  defend-
wits guilty;  and  you  can  find  them  guilty  of  murder  in  the  first
degree  or  guilty  of  murder  in  the  second  degree;  and  if  you  find
fom  the  evidence  that  the  defendants  are  guilty  but  entertain  a
doubt  as  to  whether  they  are  guilty  of  murder  in  the  first
3%  «= degree  or  guilty  of  murder  in  the  second  degree,  then  it  be-
comes  your  duty,  under  the  law,  to  give  the  defendants,  or
ifendant,  the  benetit  of  that  doubt,  and  find  them  guilty  of  the
wer  or  lesser  degree;  that  is  or  murder  in  the  second  degree;  and
fupon  the  whole  case  vou  should  entertain  a  reasonable  doubt  as  to
the defendants’  guilt.  you  should  give  them  the  benefit  of that  doubt
and find them  not  guilty.

If you  find  from  the  evidence  in  this  case  that  the  defendants  are
nilty  as  charged  in  the  indictment,  the  form  of  your  verdict  will
ie: We,  the  jury  find  the  defendants  (giving  the  names  of  the  ones
you  find  to  be  guilty)  guilty  as  charged  in  the  indictment,  of
murder  in  the  first  degree,  and  sign  it  by  one  of  your  number  as
freman.
In  the  event  that  vou  return  that  kind  of  a  verdict,  the  law
ixes the  punishment,  which  is death  by electrocution ; and  the  other
form of your  verdict  for  murder  in  the  first  degree  is:  ‘We,  the  jury,
ind  the  defendants  (naming  them)  guilty  as  charged  in  the  in-
dietment,  of  murder  in  the  first  degree,  and  fix  their  punishment  at
life  imprisonment  in  the  State  Penitentiary.”
In  other  words,
gntlemen,  it is optional  or  in  the  discretion  of  the  jury  as  to  whether
the death  penalty  or  life  imprisonment  is  inflicted,  in  the  event  you
should  find  the  defendants  guilty  of  murder  in  the  first  degree.

If you  find  the  defendants  guilty  of  murder  in  the  second  degree,
the form  of  your  verdict  will  be:  We,  the  jury,  find  the  defendants,
(naming  them)  guilty  of  murder  in  the  second  degree  and  fix  their
punishment’’—confining  them  to  the  Penitentiary  for  some

)  = period  of  years,  not  less  than  five  nor  more  than  21  years.

If  upon  the  whole  case  you  should  entertain  a  reasonable
doubt  of  the  defendants’  guilt  you  should  give  them  the  benefit  of
that doubt,  and  in  that  event  the  form  of  your  verdict  would  simply
te: We, the  jury,  find  the  defendants  not  guilty.”

Gentlemen  of  the  jury,  you  understand  that  under  this  indict-
nent, where  there  are  five  defendants  indicted  charged  with  murder
m  the  first  degree,  you  can  find  one  of  the  defendants  guilty  of

The  State  is  required  to  prove  all  of  the  material  allegations jy

this  indictment  as  in  all  criminal  cases  and  to  prove  them  to yor
fninds  beyond  a  reasonable  doubt,  and  if the  jury  should  entertain,
reasonable  doubt  as  to  any  material  allegations  in  this  indie
they  should  give  the  defendant  or  defendants  the  benefit  of thy
doubt  and  acquit  them.
A  reasonable  doubt,  gentlemen  of the jury
is  not  a  mere  possible  or  imaginary  doubt,  because  everything  rely

61

ing  to  human  affairs  and  depending  upon  moral  evidence i
open  to  some  possibie  or  imaginary  doubt,  but  it  is such,
doubt  as  would  cause  a  prudent  man  to  pause  or  hesitate ip

the  graver  transactions  of  life,  and  a  juror  is  satisfied  beyond
reasonable  doubt  if  from  a  fair  and  eandid  consideration  of all th
evidence  he  has  an  abiding  conviction  of  the  truth  of  the  charge,

The  defendants  in  this  case,  as  in  all  others  of  a  criminal  natur
where  a  person  is charged  with  a  criminal  offense,  is presumed  tok
innocent,  and  that  presumption  attends  and  protects  him  until
such  time  as  it  is  overcome  by  legal  and  competent  evidence  upm
the  part  of  the  State.  The  fact  that  an  indictment  has  been  returnd
against  these  defendants  should  not  be  considered  by  you  in dete:
mining  their  guilt  or  innocence;  they  are  presumed  to  be  innocent
of the  crime  of  which  they  are  charged  until  cach  and  every  materi
allegation  in  the  indictment  is  establishment  to  the  minds  of each
and  every  member  of  the  jury  beyond  a  reasonable  doubt.”

To  which  oral  instruction,  given  to  the  jury  by the  court,  defend
ant,  at  the  time,  duly  excepted  and  caused  his  exceptions  to  be noted
of record.

Order.

Now  on  this  the  20th  day  of  December  1919  the  above  and  fore-
going motion  for  a  new  trial  coming  on  to  be  heard  before  the  Court
and  being  presented  to  the  Court,  after  argument  of  counsel,  and  the
Court  being  fully  advised  in  the  premises,  and  after  due  consideration
of the  sume,  the  court  doth  overrule  and  deny  said  motion  for  a  new
trial;  to  which  ruling  and  action  of  the  Court  in  overruling  and
denying  said  motion  for  a  new  trial,  the  defendant,  at  the  time,  duly
excepted  and  caused  their  exceptions  to  be  noted  of  record,
Thereupon,  the  defendants  prayed  an  uppeal  from  the  judgment
and  order  of  the  Phillips  Circuit  Court  to  the  Supreme  Court  of
Arkansas,  which  said  prayed  for  an  appeal  was  by the  Court  granted,
and  time  being  asked,  60  days  was  by  the  Court  granted  from  and
ifter  November — 1919  to  the  defendants  within  which  to  prepare,

67

Motion  for  New  Trial.

Defendants,  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,  J,  E.  Knox.  Ed  Coleman
and  Paul  Hall,  jointly  and  severally  move  and  pray  the  court  to
st aside  the  verdict  of  the  jury  herein  and  grant  them  and  give  to
them  a  new  trial  herein  for  the  following  reasons:

1,

They  are  all  Negroes,  of  the  African  race,  and  were  at  the  time
of the  trial,  and  for  a  long  time  previous  thereto  had  been  citizens
of the  United  States  and  of  the  State  of  Arkansas,  and  residents
of Phillips  County ; that  the  deceased,  Clinton  Lee,  whom  the  defend-
ants  are  charged  by the  indictments  with  murdering,  was  killed  on
the Ist of October,  1919,  by some  person  unknown  to  the  defendants
ina deadly  conflict  following  a  disturbance  between  the  White  and
black  races  of  said  County  on  the  night  previous,  for  which  they
were  in no  way  responsible;  that  the  excitement  of the  white  residents
and citizens  of said  County  was  intense,  and  their  feelings  against  the
blacks,  including  the  defendants.  bitter,  active  and  persistent;  that
in the course  of  it,  some  four  or  five  white  men  and  a  large  number
of negroes  were  killed,  from  50  to  60:  that  on  or  about  said  Ist
day  of  October,  1919,  defendants  were,  along  with  many  other
negroes,  two  hundred  or  more,  taken  into  custody  by  said  whites,
carried  to  the  County  jail  and  there  kept  in  close  custody  and
confinement  until  they  were  indicted  and  put  upon  trial;  that  at
the time  of  the  returning  of  the  indictrient  and  of  said  trial,  said
«citement  and  bitterness  of feeling  among  the  Whites  of said  County
against  the  negroes,  especially  against  defendants,  was  unabated,
till  at  the  height  of  intensity:  that  this  feeling  among  the  whites
was  co-extensive  with  the  County:  that  during  their  confine-
‘8  == ment  they  were  frequently  subjected  to  torture  for  the  pur-
pose  of  extracting  from  them  admissions  of  guilt,  as  others
then  also  in  custody,  to  force  them  to  testify  against  defendants;
that  they  were  given  no  opportunity  toe  consult  with  friends  or  to
vek assistance  for  defense  or  relief  nor  were  they  even  informed  of
he charg  against  thm  until  after  their  indictment:  that  while  they
vere  thus  confined  several  hundred  white  men  of  said  County  as-
embled  at  or  near  the  Court  Hause  and  jail  for  the  purpose  of
nobbing  them,  and  were  only  prevented  from  doing  so  as  defend-
ints are informed  and  believe,  by  the  presence  of  the  U.  S.  soldiers ;
the  indictment  was  returned  on  the  29th  day  of  October  by the

the  Court  Room  and  put  on  trial  the  court  appointing  an  attorney
for  them,  before  a  jury  composed  wholly  of  white  men:  that  th
excitement  and  feeling  against  the  defendants  among  the  whites  f
said  County  was  such  that  it  was  impossible  to  obtain  any  yp.
prejudiced  jury  of  white  men  to  trv  them  and  that  no  white  jurr
being  fairly  disposed,  would  have  had  the  courage  to  acquit  them:
that  the  trial  proceeded  without  consultation  on  their  part  with  any
attorney,  without  any  witnesses  in  their  behalf  and  without  an  oppor.
unity  on  their  part  to  obtain  witnesses  or  prepare  for  defense : that po

69

evidence  was  offered
in  their  behalf;  that  they  had  no
knowledge  or  familiarity  with  court:  procedure,  had  never
been  at  a  trial  in  court  before  and  had  no  definite  idea  of
their  rights  therein  and  no  conception  of  what  steps  should  be taken
for  their  protection:  that  the  whole  course  of the  trial.  from  heginning
to  end,  occupied  about  three  fourths  of  an  hour;  that  the  jury after
hearing  the  State's  evidence  and  the  Court’s  charge  retired  and  p.
turned  immediately,  that  is.  within  about  from  three  to  six  minutes.
with  a  verdict  of  guilty  against  the  defendants.

Defendants.  Ed  Hicks.  J.  FE.  Knox.  Ed  Coleman  and  Paul  Hall
further  say,  that  no  copy  of  the  indictment  was  ever  served  upon
them,  nor  upon  any  attorney
for  them,  and  defendant  Frank
Moore  says  that  a  copy  of  said  indictment  was  delivered  to  him  on
the  day  preceeding  the  trial  but  not  48  hours  before  the  trial:  and
they  all  say  that  they  never  consented  to  waive  such  service  nor
Defendants,
requested  nor  consented  to  the  trial  without  the  same.
therefore,  sav  that  they  were  convicted  and  sentenced  to  death  with
out  due  process  of  law.

2

That  under  the  law  as  it  has  existed  for  many  vears,  the  Cir
cuit  Courts  of  the  State,  at  each  Term.  appoint  jury  Commissioner
to  select  grand  and  petit  jurors  to  serve  at  the  succeeding  term,  and
for  more  than  thirty  vears  it  has  been  the  unbroken  practice  of
said  courts  to  appoint  only  white  men  on  such  commissions,  and
of  such  commissioners  to  select  only  white  men  for  grand  and  petit
jurors  for  the  succeeding  terms.—constituting  a  discrimination,  in
the  administration  of  the  law  against  the  negroes  on  account  of theit
color  and  of  their  being  members  of  the  African  race;  and
that  if  in  the  course  of  the  court’s  proceedings,  it  hecame  or
for  talismen,  to  the
becomes  necessary
sheriff,  the  unvariable  course  is,  and  has  been,  to  summon  only
white  men;  this  practice  with  reference  to  the  selection  of  grand
and  petit  jurors  and  the  summoning  of  talismen  prevails  and  has
prevailed  in  the  Circuit  Court  of  Phillips  County  with  unbroken  uni-
formity  to  the  extent  that  no  negro  has  been  appointed  a  juy

Now  on  this  day  comes  the  defendants  by  attorneys  Murphy  &
McHaney  and  Scipio  Jones,  Esq.,  and  by  leave  of  the  Court  file
motion  for  a  new  trial  in  this  cause,  which  said  motion  coming  on
to be heard  and  the  court  being  well  and  sufficiently  advised  as  to
what  order  to  render  herein  doth  overrule  said  motion,  to  which
action  of  the  court  in  overruling  said  motion  the  defendant-  at  the
time  excepted  and  asked  that  their  exceptions  be  noted  of  record
vhich  was  done,  and  defendants  prayed  an  appeal  to  the  Supreme
Court  of  Arkansas,  which  was  granted,  and  defendants  are  allowed
60 days  within  which  to  prepare  and  file  their  bill  of  exceptions.
‘Circuit  Court  Record  “U,”  page  122,  December  20,  1919.

80

November  Term,  1919.

(Caption  Omitted.)

March  29, 1920,

This  cause  came  on  to  be  heard  upon  the  transcript  of  the  record
of  the  circuit  court  of  Phillips  county,  and  was  argued  by counsel,
on  consideration  whereof  it  is  the  opinion  «f  the  Court  that  there js
no  error  in  the  proceedings  and  judgment  of said  cireuit  court  in this
cause.

It  is therefore  considered  by the  Court  that  the  judgment  of said
circuit  court  in  this  cause  rendered  be,  and  the  same  is hereby,  in all
things,  affirmed  with  costs.

It is further  considered  that  said  appeilee  recover  of said  appellants
all  her  costs  in  this  court  in  this  cause  expended  and  have  execution
thereof.

In  the  Supreme  Court  of  Arkansas,  March  29,  1920.

No.  293.

Hicks

Vv.

STATE  OF  ARKANSAS.

Moore  et  al.

We

STATE  oF  ARKANSAS.

Opinion,

Smita,  J.:

Appellant  Frank  Hicks  was  indicted  for  murder  in  the first  de
gree,  alleged  to  have  been  committed  by shooting  one  Clinton  Lee,

“a

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL.  VS.  THE  STATE  OF  ARKANSAS,

65

43

Appellants  Frank
and  at  his  trial  was  convicted  of  that  crime.
Moore,  Ed  Hicks,  J.  K. Knox,  Ed  Coleman,  and  Paul  Hall,  were  in-
dicted  for the  same  crime,  and  were  tried  together,  and  were  all  con-
yieted  of  murder  in  the  first  degree.
Appeals  have  been  perfected
from  both  judgments,  and  as  the  questions  raised  are  substantially
the same in  each  case  we  dispose  of  both  with  one  opinion.

It is insisted  as  ground  for  reversal  in  each  case  that  appellants,
sho are all  men  of color,  were  discriminated  against  on  that  account,
in that  no  colored  man  sat,  or  was  summoned  to  serve,  upon  either
the grand  jury  which  returned  the  indictments  or  upon  the  petit
juries which  tried the cases,  and  that  no  colored  man  had  served  on
any  jury  in  Phillips  county—where  the  trials  oceurred—for  many
years.
This  assignment  of  error  is  answered  by  saying  that  the
question  was  first  raised  in  the  motion  for  a  new  trial,  and  it, there-
fore,  comes  too  late  to  be  now  considered.
Tillman  vy.  State,  121
Ark. 322;  Eastling  v.  State,  69  Ark.  189.

®

It  is  also  insisted  on  behalf  of  all  the  appellants  that  the
verdicts  are  contrary  to  the  law  and  the  evidence,  and  are  not
supported  by sufficient  evidence,  and  that  the  trials  occurred
under  such  circumstances  as  that  appellants  have  been  convicted
without  due  process  of  law.
The  facts  upon  which  these  conten-
tions are  chiefly  made  were  not  developed  at  the  trial  but  are  brought
into the  record  by affidavits  filed  in  support  of  the  motions  for  new
‘trials.
-  Diseussing  these  questions  together,  it  may  be  said  that  appellants,
together  with  many  other  men  of  their  own  race,  were  members  of an
organization  known  as  the  Farmers  Progressive  Household  Union  of
‘America.
According  to  the  affidavits  filed  in  support  of  the  motions
for new trials.  this  was  a  fraternal  and  social  organization,  organized
for the  lawful  purpose  of  promoting  the  financial  interests  of  its
members;  that  it  met  in  secret,  excluding  all  persons  except  those
tho had  been  properly  initiated,  but  according  to  the  testimony  on
half  of the  State  members  were  told  upon  their  initiation  to  expect
and prepare  for  trouble  with  the  white  people.  Members  went  armed
tothe  meetings,  and  armed  pickets  were  placed  about  the  chureh  in
thich  the  meetings  were  held  to  prevent  persons  not  members,  for-
tified  with  the  password,  from  approaching  the  building.
While
me of these  meetings  was  in  progress  an  automobile  containing  three
thite  men  passed  along  the  public  road  near  this  building,  and
topped some  forty  or  fifty  yards  from  it,  whereupon  the  pickets  fired
into the car  and  killed  one  of  the  men  in  it.

8

At  the  trials  from  which  these  appeals  come  the  following
facts  were  developed.
Early  in  the  morning  after  the  killing
of the  man  in  the  car  a  number  of  the  lodge  members,  prob-
ily as  many  as  fifty,  including  appellants,  assembled  at  or  about
houses  of  appellants  Moore,  Hall  and  Coleman,  about  a  mile
fom Elaine  and  Hoop  Spur,  where  the  shooting  of  the  night  before
had occurred.
All,or  practically all,  of the  members  were  armed,  and
appellant  Moore  made  the  statement  that  they  would  kill  the  white
people who  came  there.
All  of  this  is admitted,  but  it  is explained
that the members  had  gathered  only  to  resist  an  attack  being  made

Ol)

44

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL.  VS.  THE  STATE  OF  ARKANSAS,
on  themselves  and  that  they  intended  to  kill  the  white  men  whom
they  expected  to  come  only  to  prevent  the  white  men  from  killing
them.
Sometime  between  11  a.  m.  and  1  p.  m.  firing  near  Elaine  or
Hoop  Spur  was  heard,  whereupon  appellants,  with  a  number  of
others,  fell  into  line,  and  proceeded  to  march  toward  Elaine,  Moore
having  said,  as  they  fell  into  line,  that  some  of  their  members  Were
being  attacked  and  that  they  would  go  and  help  them  fight.  As
they  marched  along  by twos  and  fours  they  crossed  a  railroad,  and,
as  they  did  so,  they  observed,  at  the  home  of  one  McCoy,  a  white
man,  about  a  quarter  of  a  mile  away,  a  number  of  white  men  stand.
ing  in  the  road  at  McCoy's  house  or  seated  in  the  cars  which  had
stopped  there  on  the  road-side.  When  they  observed  the  white  men
appellant  Frank  Hicks  said  he  would  shoot  at  them.
Tle  knelt,  took
aim  and  fired  two  shots,  one  of  which  struck  Clinton  Lee,  who  died
just  after  he  was  carried  into  McCoy's  house.  These  shots  were  fired
from  a  high-powered  rifle,  and  at  such  distance  away  that  some  of
the  white  men  standing  near  Lee  stated  that  thev  did  not  hear
S4
the  report  of the  gun  which  killed  him.  This  party  of white
men  consisted  of  officers  who  had  come  to  Elaine  to  effect  the
arrest  of  the  men  who  had  killed  the  man  in  the  automobile  the
night  before.
There  was  testimony  to  the  effect  that  when  Hicks
said  he  would  shoot  a  member  of  his  party  told  him  not  to  do g0,
but  no  one  made  any  attempt  to  restrain  him,
After  this  shooting
the  party  dispersed,  and  during  the  excitement  of  the  next  few  days
two  other  white  men  were  killed  and  a  number  of  negroes.
It  is  now  insisted  that  heeause  of  the  incidents  developed  at  the
trial  and  those  recited  in  the  motions  for  new  trials,  and  the  excite
ment  and  feeling  growing  out  of  them.  that  no  fair  trial  was  had, or
could  have  been  had,  and  that  the  trial  did  not,  therefore,  constitute
due  process  of law.
It  is  admitted,  however,  that  eminent  counsel  was  appointed  to
defend  appellants,  and  no  attempt  is  made  to  show  that  a  fair  and
impartial  trial  was  not  had,  except  as  an  inference  from  the  facts
stated  above,  the  insistence  being  that  a  fair  trial  was  impossible
under  the  circumstances  stated.
We  are  unable,  however,  to  say  that  this  must  necessarily  have
been  the  case.
The  trials  were  had  according  to  the  law.  the  jury
was  correctly  charged  as  to  the  law  of  the  case,  and  the  testimony
is  legally  sufficient  to  support  the  verdiets  returned.
We  cannot,
therefore,  in  the  face  of  this  aflirmative  showing,  assume  that  the
trial  was  an  empty  ceremony,  conducted  for  the  purpose  only  of
appearing  to  comply  with  the  requirements  of  the  law,  when
85
they  were  not  in  fact  being  complied  with.

As  to  the  appellants  Frank  Moore,  Ed  Hicks,  J.  FE.  Knox,
Ed  Coleman,  and  Paul  Hall,  it  is  insisted  that  the  testimony  does
not  sufficiently  connect  them  with  the  act  of  Frank  Hieks  in  firing
the  fatal  shot  to  make  them  criminally  responsible  for  that  act,  The
law  of the  subject  was  properly  declared,  and  we  think  the  testimony
of  the  numerous  witnesses  who  were  examined  and  cross-examined
at  the  trial  supports  the  finding  that  those  appellants  were  present

According  to  these  witnesses  those  appellants  were
Hicks  himself.
all armed,  and  before  leaving  the  place  from  which  they  started  the
of  going  to  Elaine  to  fight  the  white  men  found  there  was
‘announced,  and  we  think  this  testimony  warranted  the  jury  in  find-

In the  case  of  appellant  Frank  Hicks  it  is  insisted  that  the  judg-
ment  must  be  reversed  because  of  the  defective  verdict.
'The  verdict
gs found  in  the  bill  of exceptions  was  originally  written  in  typewrit-
ing as  follows:  “We,  the  jury,  find  the  defendant,  Frank  Hicks,
Over  this  verdict  has  been
guilty  as  charged  in  the  indictment.”
*  jnterlined,  between  the  words  “guilty”  and  “as”,  the  follow-
ing words:  “of  murder  in  the  first  degree.”  so  that  the  verdict
as  interlined  reads:  “We.  the  jury,  find  the  defendant,  Frank
Hicks.  guilty  of  murder  in  the  first  degree  as  charged  in  the  indict-
ment.”  On  the  margin  of  the  page  of  the  transcript  on  which  this
verdict  appears  is  the  following  certificate  made  and  signed  by  the
trial  judge:  “The  interlineation  made  in  this  verdict  was  made
before  I signed  the  bill  of exceptions.  and  correctly  shows  the  verdict
as it was  returned  by the  jury.
J.  M.  Jackson.  Cireuit  Judge.”  The
judgment  of  the  court  also  sets  out  in  full  the  verdict  returned,  and
the verdict  as  it  is  there  recorded  conforms  to  the  certificate  of  the
trial judge set  out  above.

8

It is true  that  in  the  cases  of  Johnson  v.  State.  84  Ark.  95,  and
Hobbs  v.  State,  86  Ark.  360,  and  Bridger  v.  State.  122  Ark.  391.  we
ignored  as  unauthorized  certain  interlineations  made  with  a  lead
pencil  for  the  reason.  there  stated,  that  the  interlineations  were  un-
vaplained  and  unauthenticated  and  apparently  made  without  au-
thority.  But  it  has  not  been  decided  that  this  court  will  necessarily
ignore  interlineations  appearing  in  a  bill  of  exceptions  or  a  tran-
vript.  Upon  the  contrary,  interlineations  may  be  a  proper  part  of
the record,  and  will  be  so  treated  by  us  unless  it  appears  such  inter-
Tere  we  have  the  certificate
-lineations  were  not  properly  authorized,
of the  trial  judge  saving  that  the  interlineation  was  made  before  he
had  approved  or  signed  the  bill  of  exeeptions.  and,  in  addition,  we
have  in the  judgment  proper  a  record  of  the  verdict  which  shows  it
tobe  in proper  form.

8

We  have  given  these  cases  the  careful  consideration  which
their  importance  required,  but  our  consideration  is necessarily
limited  to  those  matters  which  are  properly  brought  before
ws  for  review,  and  as  no  error  has  been  made  to  appear  in  either
case  the judgments  must  be affirmed.

It  is so  ordered.

and  by the  Court  taken  under  advisement.

April  19,  1920.

In  the  Supreme  Court  of  Arkansas.

FrRaNkK  Moore,  Ep  Hicks,  J.  E.  Kyvox.  Ep  COLEMAN,  and  Paty
Haxz,  Appellants,
Vs.
Tue  Stare  or  ARKANSAS,  Appellee.

Petition  for  Rehearing.

Appellants  respectfully  pray  the  Court  to  grant  them  a  rehearing

herein,  and  says:

1.  That  the  Court  is  in  error  in  its  conclusion  that  their  claim
that  the  selection  and  organization  of  the  grand  jury,  which  re.
turned  the  indictment  against  them  and  the  petit  jury  that  con.
victed  them,  composed  wholly  of  white  men.  was  a  discrimination
on  account  of  their  color—assigned  as  a  ground  of  the  motion  for
a  new  trial,  comes  too  late  to  be  considered.
Under  the  rules  of  practice  in  this  state,  as  previously  announced
by  this  Court,  they  could  have  raised  the  question  as  to  the  grand
jury  at  no  earlier  stage  of  the  proceeding,  having  been  denied  all
opportunity  to  be  present  or  make  objection  to  such  grand  jury or
any  of its  members,  at  the  time  of  its  organization,

2.  The  Court  is  in  error  in  its  conclusion  that  the  fact  that  the
“Farmers  Progressive  Household  Union  of  America,”  was.  accord.
ing  to  the  affidavits  filed  in  support  of  the  motion  for  a  new  trial,
a  fraternal  and  social  organization,  organized  for  the  lawful
90
purpose  of  promoting  the  financial  interests  of  its  members,
who  were  admonished.  on  their  initiation,  that  attempts  had
been  made  to  break  up  the  lodge  by white  people,  and  to  expect  and
prepare  for  trouble  with  the  white  people.
All  this  occurred  in  the  evidence  of  witnesses  for  the  State.  mem-
bers  of  the  organization,—not  one  word  of  it  or  about  it  either  in
the  motion  for  a  new  trial  or  in  any  affidavit  in  support  of  it

3.  The  Court  is  in  error  in  concluding  and  holding  that  appel-
lents’  insistence  “that  because  of  the  incidents  developed  at  the  trial
and  those  recited  in  the  motion  for  a  new  trial.  and  the  excitement
and  feeling  growing  out  of  them,  no  fair  trial  was  had,  or  could.

47

have  been  had,  and  that  the  trial  did  not,  therefore,  constitute  due
rocess  of law,’’  was  not  necessarily  the  case.
If the  facts  were  as  recited—undisputed  facts—it  is manifest  that
appellants  were  not  accorded  due  process  of  law, and  that  a  fair and
impartial  trial  was  not  had,—was,  under  the  circumstances,  impos-
able.

4.  The  Court  is  in  error  in  its  conclusion  that  the  trial  was  had
according  to  law,  the  testimony  legally  sufficient  to  support  the  ver-
diet,  and  that  it  must  necessarily  appear  that  due  process  of  law  was
denied,  and  “that  the  trial  was  an  empty  ceremony,  conducted  for
the  purpose  only  of  appearing  to  comply  with  the  requirements  of

the  law,”  to  entitle  appellants  to  a  new  trial.

9]

Where  the  circumstances  brought  out  on  motion  for  a  new
trial  or  elsewhere  bring  the  fairness  and  impartiality  into
anything  like  serious  question,  the  judgment  of  conviction  should
te reversed.
In  the  language  of  the  appellate  court  in  Rutherford
vy. US.  258  Fed.,  page  S63,  “It  is not  enough  to  justify  a conviction
He  has  a  right  to  be  tried  in  accord-
that  the  defendant  is  guilty.
ance  With  the  rules  of  law.
The  defendants  in  this  ease  did  not
have  the  temperate  and  impartial  trial  to  which  they  were  entitled,
and for  that  reason  the  judgment  is reversed.”

The  Court  is  in  error  in  its  apparent  conclusion  that  the  matters
#t  up  in  the  motion  for  a  new  trial  are  not  properly  brought  before
it for review,  and  that  the  judgment  must,  therefore,  be  affirmed.
If it be  conceded  that  the  matters  relating  to  the  grand  and  the
petfit juries  were  raised  too  late,  still  the  other  matters  set  up  in  the
motion  which  are  undisputed,  are  not  too  late,  if  true,  demand  a
reversal,  and  should  be  considered.
Moreover,  while  counsel  ap-
pointed  to  represent  appellants,  may,  with  reference  to  learning  and
capacity,  be  justly  considered  eminent,  they  were  members  of  the
local  bar,  dominated  by the  unfriendly  and  violent  spirit  that  domi-
nated  the  whole  proceeding,  and  scrutinizing  search  of  the  record
will fail  to  show  any  real  effort  on  their  part  to  afford  appellants  any
real  protection.
Ilas  it  ever  before  occurred  that  a  defendant
charged  with  so  grave  a  Grime  has  been  denied  all  opportu-
nity to  get  witnesses,  to  prepare  for  defense,  tried,  so  to speak,
in  the  midst  of  a  populace  excited  and  enraged  against  him,
by a jury  whose  members  were  of  a  different  race  and  color  from  his
won,  and  one  of  whose  members  he  is charged  of  having  murdered
could  find  no  relief  in  the  appellate  court?
Such  a  ease  will  thus
go into judicial  history,  if it must  retain  its  present  statue

Appellants  therefore  pray  a  rehearing  be  granted  them  herein,

and that  the  judgment  against  them  be  reversed.

SCIPIO  A.  JONES,
MURPHY  &  McHANEY,

Smira, J.:

Appellant  Prank  Pieks  was  indicted  for  murder  in.  the  first  de
gree,  alleged  to  have  been  committed  by  shooting  one  Clinton  Lee,
and  at  his  trial  was  convicted  of  that  crime.

State  of  Arkansas,

Proclamation.

To all  to  whom  these  presents  shall  come,  Greetings:

Know  ve,  that  whereas,  J.  i.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman,  Paul  Hall,
Frank  Hicks,  Ed  Hicks  and  Frank  Moore,  were  convicted  at  the
November,  1919,  Term  of  the  Phillips  County  Cirenit  Court  of  the
crime  of  murder  in  the  first  degree,  and  sentenced  by said  court  to
death  by  electrocution,  and  their  cases  being  appealed  to  the  Su
preme  Court  of  Arkansas,  the  pendency  of  which  appeal  suspended
execution  bevond  the  date  fixed  by  the  said  trial  court.  and  said
judgments  of  conviction  being  by  said  court  affirmed,  it  now  be-
comes  my  duty  as  Governor  of  the  State  of  Arkansas,  under  Section
3262  of  Crawford  and  Moses’  Digest  of  the  statutes  of  Arkansas,  to
ix the date  of  their  execution,  and  each  of  them;

Now,  therefore,  1,  Thomas  C.  McRae,  Governor  of  the  State  of
Arkansas,  acting  in  my  oflicial  capacity,  do  hereby  and  herein  fix
the date  for  the  execution  of  the  said  J,  E.  Knox,  Ed  Coleman.  Paul
Hall, Frank  Hicks,  Ed  Hicks  and  Frank  Moore,  and  each  of  them.
tobe on Friday.  the  10th  day  of  June,  1921.

In testimony  whereof,  | have  hereunto  set  my  hand  and  caused  to
beaflixed  the  Great  Seal  of State,  in  the  Executive  Chamber,  at  Little
Rock,  Arkansas,  on  this  the  twenty-ninth  day  of  April,  1921.

[SEAL.  |

By the  Governor:

IRA  C.  HOPPER,

Secretary  of State.

THOMAS  C.  McRAEF,

Governor.

Resolution.

It  has  been  brought  to  the  attention  of  the  Richard  L.  Kiteher
Post  #31,  American  Legion,  Helena,  Arkansas,  that  the  Governe
is  contemplating  commuting  the  sentence  of  four  of  the  negme,

er

i

~

-

ae

*  MLA

FRANK  MOORE  ET  AL,  VS.  F.  H.  DEMPSEY,  ETC.

77

who  are  now  under  death  sentences  for  their  participation  in the
Elaine  Riot,  to  lesser  sentences,  and  we,  the  members  of  this  post
feel that  any  action  toward  this  end  by the  Governor,  would  do more
harm  in  the  commamity  and  breed.  Jawlessness,  as  well  as  disregard
for  constituted  authority,  as  at  the  time  of  this  race  riot  the  mem-
pers  of  this  Post  were  called  upon  to  go  to  Hoop  Spur  and  Elaine
y  protect  life  and  property,  and  in  compliance  with  this  request,
there  were  two  American  Legion  members  killed  and  one  seriously
injured,  besides  the  other  non-members  who  also  perished,  and
when  the  guilty  negroes  were  apprehended,  a  solemn  promise  was
given  by  the  leading  citizens  of  the  community,  that  if  these  guilty
parties  were  not  lynched,  and  let  the  luw  take  its  course,  that  justice
vould  be  done  and  the  majesty  of  the  law  upheld.

The  twelve  negroes  now  under  sentence  of  death,  but  whose  sen-
ences  are  suspended  —— accovint  of  court  procedure,  and  six  of  these
negro  caves  have  —  taken  to  the  Supreme  Court  of the  United  States,
whie  court  declined  to  review.
‘The  other  six  eases,  whose  original
trials  were  reversed  and  new  trials  given  them,  were  convicted,  and
their  cases  were  appealed  to  the  Supreme  Court  of  the  State  and  at-
orneys  of  their  own  selection  were  permitted  to  handle  their  cases,
Now  therefore  be  it  resolved  by  this  Post  assembled  on  this  the
Ith  daw  of  Oetober,  1920,  that  we  most  earnestly  protest  against
the commutation  of  ane  of  the  sentences  of  these  twelve  negroes  con-
vieted  of  murder  in  the  Flaine  riot  of  October  1919,  their  having  re-
ceived  a  fair  trial  and  ~~ proven  guilty,  and  the  leniency  of  the  court
was  shown  in  the  balance  of  the  cases  tried,  these  being  the  ring
leaders  and  guilty  murderers,  and  that  law  and  order  will  be  vin-
dicated  and  «  solemn  promise  kept.

Be  it  further  resolved  that  a  com-ittee  of  four  he  appointed  by
This  Committee  is  hereby  empowered  to
the  Post  Commander.
represent  this  Post  at  a  conference,  or  several  conferences,  with  the
overnor  of  Arkansas  and  to  take  such  steps  as  they  may  deem
necessary  to  carry  out  the  wishes  of  this  resolution  and  leaving  noth-
ing  undone  to  have  these  sentences  carried  out.
This  committee  to
report  in  full  to  the  next  meeting  of  this  post.

Passed  wnanimously  8:30  P.M.  October  19.

This

It  defines  a  writ  of  mandamus,
